  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_296176c4-4f74-4875-b3b2-baef715a172c'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/135097">قصة قصيرة جدا تنهيها فى ربع ساعة ولكنه مرهقة ذهنية بعض الشئ، لكنى لم أفهم نهايتها أو المغزى منها!
ربما كانت نهايتها غير قريبة من ذهنى بسبب إختلاف الثقافة أو إختلاف المفهوم الدينى لها ربما؟

can any help please?</a>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/30302280-samira-elytess"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1559687121i/30302280._UX100_CR0,16,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/30302280-samira-elytess">Samira Elytess</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent"><br/>This story tells us an enigma. We are the creator and the creation at the same time.<br/><br/>As humans evolve, they become "God" or Creator.<br/>Each story shows hu<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent"><br />This story tells us an enigma. We are the creator and the creation at the same time.<br /><br />As humans evolve, they become "God" or Creator.<br />Each story shows humans evolution in increments. <br />( We evolve from flesh and blood to disembodied mind/energy/nano-tech particle cyborgs)<br /><br />First story, humans are on Earth.<br />Second story, humans are in Space.<br />Third story, humans are outside of the Galaxy.<br />Fourth story, humans got rid of their bodies and are Minds.As disembodied individuals each of them have retained their personality or identity.<br />Fifth story,all individual minds lose their identity as they merge and form "GOD" or the Cosmic AC computer<br /><br />The cycle of life starts all over again when the computer=God=Merged Individual Minds, manifests itself in the universe as big bang:"Let there be light" <br />In which, time, space, and life is created and entropy starts all over again.Thus, the cyclical pattern of the universe continues and so does humans' saga.<br /><br />The question is: Which came first, the chicken or the egg? Cosmic AC/God or humans inventing the Cosmic AC/God?<br />Are we trapped in a computer glitch that with each big bang birth we all play the same story plots? <br /><br />It helps to have mystical and transhumanisim backgrounds to understand the story.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer561464084" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12054105138264140465">What's the point in giving you a summery for a 4,000-word short story? So here's what I'll do instead: I'll give you a lesson in physics. Sound good? Cool. Ever hear of the second law of thermodynamics? Yes? But you have no clue what it means? Okay, well we can work with that. Here's what Wikipedia has on the subject: <br/><i>
  <a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics' rel='nofollow noopener'>The second law of thermodynamics states that the entropy of an isolated system never decreases, because isolated systems spontaneously evolve towards thermodynamic equilibrium—t</a></i></span>
  <span id="freeText12054105138264140465" style="display:none">What's the point in giving you a summery for a 4,000-word short story? So here's what I'll do instead: I'll give you a lesson in physics. Sound good? Cool. Ever hear of the second law of thermodynamics? Yes? But you have no clue what it means? Okay, well we can work with that. Here's what Wikipedia has on the subject: <br /><i>
  <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" rel="nofollow noopener">The second law of thermodynamics states that the entropy of an isolated system never decreases, because isolated systems spontaneously evolve towards thermodynamic equilibrium—the state of maximum entropy. </a>
</i><br /><br />Pretty simple, right? What do you mean you don't understand entropy? Of course you understand entropy, I assure you. Every time you see a sand castle crumble away in the wind, every time your car breaks down on the road, every time somebody you know dies and is gone forever... you are seeing entropy in motion. Entropy is the measure of disorder in a system... a measure of chaos. The second law of thermodynamics tells us that entropy in a closed system, be it your sand castle, car, or life... never decreases, it will only ever increase to maximum.<br /><br />But here's the thing, it's not really a law by any means. Nothing in all of actual physics states that this must be, physics actually says it's perfectly possible for random sand molecules to be blown in a way where they land and form a perfect sandcastle (instead of making one crumble away). So why not? Why don't we see things like this happen? Statistics. Yes, the second law of thermodynamics is actually a statistical principle; entropy could indeed decrease in a system (any system like this would be considered a perpetual motion machine), but it's just not statistically likely. <br /><br />Meaning a sand castle could coincidently form by the winds blowing dust randomly..., but it won't. It won't because the statistical likelihood of the wind and molecules to just happen to be in the right position and velocity for this sand castle to just appear is so slim, I do not have enough memory in my computer to type out all the zeros I need to put behind the odds of it happening (1 in 1,000,000,000,000,000,...).<br /><br />This statistical principle is so powerful and universal, we call it a law. It explains a lot of things in our universe. Before we knew about the second law of thermodynamics, it used to bother physicist that we could move in any direction in space (up, down, forward, backward) but we could only move in one direction in time. Space and time being so closely linked, why was it we could only move forward in time? It’s because entropy always increases that we move forward in time (and never back in time), in a way time is just a measure of increasing entropy. We only go from one moment to another as entropy increases. <br /><br />Ahhh, I see what you are thinking. Yes, our universe is a closed system, and yes that does mean <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" rel="nofollow noopener"> it will die, . </a>You think it's a little sad that in the end chaos will always win? I don't think so, after all life is beautiful don't you think? Life is amazing in that it can bring a little bit of order in this chaotic world, but funny enough, without entropy there would be no life. So I don't think it's sad, it's just the natural state of affairs that we will grow old, live our lives fighting entropy, and we will die. it's really quite beautiful. <br /><br />If you want to read a book that asks the questions: what if we could defeat entropy? What if we could win? What would happen? Read this book. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12054105138264140465" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/419567901?book_show_action=true">Sep 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmad Sharabiani" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani">Ahmad Sharabiani</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" " href="/book/show/53929198-the-last-question">review of another edition</a>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>,

              <span id="reviewTextContainer419567901" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7785913867845097923">The Last Question (When the World Ends), Isaac Asimov<br/><br/>A science fiction short story by American writer Isaac Asimov. <br/><br/>The story deals with the development of a series of computers called Multivac and their relationships with humanity through the courses of seven historic settings, beginning in 2061. <br/><br/>In each of the first six scenes a different character presents the computer with the same question; namely, how the threat to human existence posed by the heat death of the universe can be averted. <br/><br/>The</span>
  <span id="freeText7785913867845097923" style="display:none">The Last Question (When the World Ends), Isaac Asimov<br /><br />A science fiction short story by American writer Isaac Asimov. <br /><br />The story deals with the development of a series of computers called Multivac and their relationships with humanity through the courses of seven historic settings, beginning in 2061. <br /><br />In each of the first six scenes a different character presents the computer with the same question; namely, how the threat to human existence posed by the heat death of the universe can be averted. <br /><br />The question was: <i>"How can the net amount of entropy of the universe be massively decreased?"</i><br /><br />This is equivalent to asking: <i>"Can the workings of the second law of thermodynamics (used in the story as the increase of the entropy of the universe) be reversed?"</i> <br /><br />Multivac's only response after much "thinking" is: <b>
  <i>"INSUFFICIENT DATA FOR MEANINGFUL ANSWER."</i>
</b> ...<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز بیست و پنجم ماه دسامبر سال 2008میلادی<br /><br />داستان کوتاه «آخرین سئوال» پیشتر در مجموعه ی داستانهای کوتاه «آسیموف» چاپ شده است، و جناب آقای: «حسین شهرابی» هم، ترجمه این داستان را در سال 1385هجری خورشیدی و در آکادمی فانتزی، و در آدرس زیر برای خوانشگران<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.fantasy.ir/news/story/the-last-question">http://www.fantasy.ir/news/story/the-...</a><br />آراسته است<br /><br />نمونه متن: «الکساندر آدِل و برترام لوپوف، دو تا از مهندسان کشیکِ ساعی و وفادارِ «مولتی‌واک» بودند؛ آن دو هم همان اندازه خبر داشتند پشت چهره‌ ی چشمک‌زن و سرد و پرسروصدای آن کامپیوتر غول‌آسا، که اندازه ‌اش چندکیلومتری می‌شد، چه می‌گذرد، که بقیه ‌ی انسان‌های عادی؛ هر چند که دست‌کم آنها تصوری مبهم از طرح کلّیِ آن رله‌ ها و مدارها داشتند، که مدت‌ها بود دیگر هیچ بنی ‌بشری به تنهایی از کل آن سر درنمی‌آورد؛ مدتی می‌شد که کار مولتی‌واک به شیوه‌ ی تنظیم و تصحیح خودکار بود؛ در واقع، می‌بایست همین طور هم می‌بود؛ چرا که انسان دیگر نه از پس آن برمی‌آمد با سرعت لازم کامپیوتر را تصحیح و تنظیم کند، و نه حتا کار را با دقت لازم انجام بدهد؛ به همین دلیل، آدل و لوپوف به این غول عظیم ‌الجثه فقط کمک‌های سطحی و دم ‌دستی می‌رساندند؛ یعنی کاری را می‌کردند که هر انسان دیگری هم از پسش برمی‌آمد: اطلاعات را به کامپیوتر می‌دادند، سئوالات را متناسب با مقتضیاتِ کامپیوتر تنظیم می‌کردند، جواب‌هایی را هم که کامپیوتر می‌داد ترجمه می‌کردند؛ با وجود این، مطمئناً این دو و بقیه‌ ی افرادی که مثل این دو بودند، مستحق فخر و شکوهی بودند که در اصل از آن مولتی‌واک بود؛ چندین دهه می‌شد که مولتی‌واک در طراحیِ فضاناوها و تعیینِ مسیرِ موشک‌ها کمک کرده بود و انسان را یاری رسانده بود تا به ماه و مریخ و ناهید دست یابد، اما از این که گذشت، منابع ناچیز زمین دیگر فایده ‌ای برای ناوها نداشت؛ چنین سفرهای طولانی‌مدتی نیاز به صرفِ انرژیِ بسیار داشت؛ زمین زودتر از این، زغال‌سنگ و اورانیومش را با بازده بالا استخراج کرده بود و دیگر چیزی از آن نمانده بود؛ اما اندک ‌اندک مولتی‌واک آموخت تا به سئوالات بغرنج‌تر پاسخ‌های اساسی‌تر بدهد؛ روز 14، ماه مه سال 2061میلادی بود که نظریه، بدل به حقیقت گشت؛ در آن تاریخ، انرژی خورشید را ذخیره، و تبدیل به دیگر انواع انرژی کردند، و مستقیماً در تمام سیاره از آن بهره گرفتند؛ همه ‌ی زمینی‌ها، زغال‌هایی را که می‌سوخت، و اورانیوم‌هایی را که دچار شکافت هسته ‌ای می‌شد، خاموش کردند، و کلیدی را زدند که همه چیز را به ایستگاهی کوچک، با قطر یکی‌دو کیلومتر، متصل می‌کرد، و ایستگاه هم در فاصله ‌ی نصف ماه تا زمین دور زمین می‌چرخید؛ دیگر در زمین همه چیز را پرتوهای نامریی آفتاب راه می‌انداخت؛ گذشتِ هفت روز آنقدر کفایت نکرده بود که شکوهِ این واقعه را زایل کند و آدل و لوپوف سرآخر تصمیم گرفتند تا از مردم کناره بگیرند و جایی هم‌دیگر را ببینند که عقل هیچ کسی قد ندهد، تا آن‌جا دنبال‌شان بگردد؛ یعنی در اتاقک‌های متروک زیرِ زمین، که بخش‌هایی از بدنه‌ ی مدفون و مقتدرِ مولتی‌واک خود را می‌نمایاند؛ مولتی‌واک هم که بدون مراقبت و در حال سکون، داده‌ها را با تِک‌تِکی رخوت‌آور منظم می‌کرد، داشت از تعطیلاتش استفاده می‌کرد، و این دو پسر هم قدردانِ این قضیه بودند؛ و البته در ابتدا هیچ منظور اذیت کردن آن را نداشتند.»؛ پایان نقل<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 15/06/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="7785913867845097923" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer16840196708784894163">This is certainly classic science fiction. A cosmic question that might not be ever solved. It’s even grander than the Ultimate Question of Life, the Universe, and Everything, to which we know the answer (42!) but not the question itself.<br/><br/>Here we know the question - can the entropy leading to eventual, billions-of-years-in-the-future end of the Universe be reversed? Can the supercomputer answer that? <br/><br/><i>
  <b>"THERE IS AS YET INSUFFICIENT DATA FOR A MEANINGFUL ANSWER."</b>
</i><br/><br/>I can’t say my little human mind </span>
  <span id="freeText16840196708784894163" style="display:none">This is certainly classic science fiction. A cosmic question that might not be ever solved. It’s even grander than the Ultimate Question of Life, the Universe, and Everything, to which we know the answer (42!) but not the question itself.<br /><br />Here we know the question - can the entropy leading to eventual, billions-of-years-in-the-future end of the Universe be reversed? Can the supercomputer answer that? <br /><br /><i>
  <b>"THERE IS AS YET INSUFFICIENT DATA FOR A MEANINGFUL ANSWER."</b>
</i><br /><br />I can’t say my little human mind that has a lifespan dwarfed by that of a mayfly on a galactic scale can really feel the existential anguish of the thought that countless billions of years from now the Universe may just cease. There are certain matters that lack relevance due to their sheer cosmic enormousness.<br /><br />But still, the answer in this one is lovely, and exactly what I thought it would be.<br /><br />The story is here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://youcanscience.com/wp-content/uploads/2015/10/LQ.pdf">http://youcanscience.com/wp-content/u...</a><br /><br />Classic.</span>

    <a title="Tyler Bair" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23715571-tyler-bair"><img alt="Tyler Bair" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1439265532p2/23715571.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/831612036?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tyler Bair" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tyler Bair" href="/user/show/23715571-tyler-bair">Tyler Bair</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23715571-tyler-bair?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer831612036" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer871308492665060067"><img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1384287395ra/6837733.gif' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/> <br/><b>JUST GOOGLE IT AND READ IT!!!</b> A very quick 15 minute read and easily one of the best sci-fi short stories I've ever read. Whatever their literary tastes, anyone is capable of thoroughly enjoying this great work by one of the greatest sci-fi authors of all time! The last two sentences will leave your mind more blown than <br/><s>a Chippendales stripper</s><br/><s>anything in or remotely involved with a film directed by Michael Bay</s><br/><s>Lindsey Lohan’s stockpile of cocaine</s><br/><s>the proportions of human stupidity</s><br/><s>the candles on a</s></span>

Whatever their literary tastes, anyone is capable of thoroughly enjoying this great work by one of the greatest sci-fi authors of all time! <span id="freeTextContainer17695218483835651333">A short story about…entropy? A reader might question how exciting such a tale can be!<br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1572716030i/28392983._SX540_.jpg' alt='Image result for asimov last question' width='542' height='305' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/> <br/>In “The Last Question,” Asimov presents nearly immortal humankind facing the end of all existence, “The last question was asked for the first time, half in jest, on May 21, 2061, at a time when humanity first stepped into the light. The question came about as a result of a five dollar bet over highballs, and it happened this way ...” Asimov tells a story that stretches billions of years into the future with bot</span>
  <span id="freeText17695218483835651333" style="display:none">A short story about…entropy? A reader might question how exciting such a tale can be!<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1572716030i/28392983._SX540_.jpg" alt="Image result for asimov last question" width="542" height="305" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br> <br>In “The Last Question,” Asimov presents nearly immortal humankind facing the end of all existence, “The last question was asked for the first time, half in jest, on May 21, 2061, at a time when humanity first stepped into the light. The question came about as a result of a five dollar bet over highballs, and it happened this way ...” Asimov tells a story that stretches billions of years into the future with both characteristic seriousness and a nod to the comical. At that point, even if the question is answered, but man is no longer around, is the question relevant? Great ending! 3.5 stars </span>
  <a data-text-id="17695218483835651333" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1518732862" />
    <a title="Kels" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28386116-kels"><img alt="Kels" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1466351542p2/28386116.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1518732862?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kels" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kels" href="/user/show/28386116-kels">Kels</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook " href="/book/show/18299452-the-last-question">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28386116-kels?shelf=tr">tr</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28386116-kels?shelf=books-that-linger">books-that-linger</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28386116-kels?shelf=futuristic">futuristic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28386116-kels?shelf=shorties">shorties</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28386116-kels?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28386116-kels?shelf=short-read">short-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28386116-kels?shelf=i-did-not-see-that-one-coming">i-did-not-see-that-one-coming</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28386116-kels?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28386116-kels?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/28386116-kels?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1518732862" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11739452521696783319">Our Sun will support life for billions of years, but what happens when it burns out? Perhaps future technology will enable us to seek out the safety of distant stars and habitat new planets, but then eventually those stars will die too. So is life as we know it, and all of mankind, destined to be doomed? <i>Is it possible to reverse entropy? </i> <br/><br/>This short story starts off in the year 2061 (keep in mind this was written in 1956) and follows the evolution of man and the subsequent advancement of techno</span>
  <span id="freeText11739452521696783319" style="display:none">Our Sun will support life for billions of years, but what happens when it burns out? Perhaps future technology will enable us to seek out the safety of distant stars and habitat new planets, but then eventually those stars will die too. So is life as we know it, and all of mankind, destined to be doomed? <i>Is it possible to reverse entropy? </i> <br /><br />This short story starts off in the year 2061 (keep in mind this was written in 1956) and follows the evolution of man and the subsequent advancement of technology throughout the ages. Split into seven sections, each story features a different POV that takes you further into the depths of space and time, racing towards the end of humanity, and ends with the resounding question, <i>How exactly may entropy be reversed?</i> growing ever more important. This question, propelled by man's innate desire to live forever, is oft repeated, yet never is there enough data to give a "meaningful answer" by the pansophical Multivac, a self-learning machine capable of evolving and expanding itself into the universe, which humans have now found themselves increasingly dependent on.<br /><br />The world Asimov creates is simply incredible, and downright ingenious. Even today, <i>The Last Question</i> rivals the ingenuity of science fiction novels, and transforms speculative fiction into something more thought provoking and profound. It is daring, and I found myself growing more and more transfixed as the story progressed, and the imposing, unanswered question became critically relevant.<br /><br />So, has Man been doomed from the start? Can entropy ever be reversed? And will the answer come soon enough before life as we know it cease to exist? <br /><br />The answer will surprise you.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11739452521696783319" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_505997088" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/505997088" />
    <a title="Sindhuja" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6188896-sindhuja"><img alt="Sindhuja" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/505997088?book_show_action=true">Jan 14, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sindhuja" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sindhuja" href="/user/show/6188896-sindhuja">Sindhuja</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6188896-sindhuja?shelf=worth-a-re-read-or-maybe-two">worth-a-re-read-or-maybe-two</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer505997088" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1757973476326701939">A review of this tale will not make sense for three reasons:<br/><br/>1) The unfolding of a certain twist in this short story is done with the last few words of this ~4000 word long story.<br/>2) A review of any form will sweeten the pill - the crux of what Asimov is trying to get at. <br/>3) Two is a reason in the first place because, it's simply impossible to describe this tale without giving away spoilers. <br/><br/>So read this after you read 'The Last Question'. <br/>That said, I can give you this: This tale leaves room for </span>
  <span id="freeText1757973476326701939" style="display:none">A review of this tale will not make sense for three reasons:<br /><br />1) The unfolding of a certain twist in this short story is done with the last few words of this ~4000 word long story.<br />2) A review of any form will sweeten the pill - the crux of what Asimov is trying to get at. <br />3) Two is a reason in the first place because, it's simply impossible to describe this tale without giving away spoilers. <br /><br />So read this after you read 'The Last Question'. <br />That said, I can give you this: This tale leaves room for a lot of inferences (That's right. not just one, but many) and interpretations by the reader and chances are, all of them hold true. For starters certain Jewish theologies(obviously) that was imparted as life went by in the life of an Indian [This story has nothing to do with India. The reviewer happens to be an Indian.] and their Hindu counterpart that many Indians grew up with, the inflated sense of importance that one gives oneself over the greater cause, the three laws of robotics AND the zeroeth law, will be of relevance to the story. There are a few more topics that were just touched in the story. Like any other Asimov's work, the story does not splash in, any of these terms though. The story perse is a bunch of conversations between laymen across different times in the future.<br /><br />The story also mocks the co-existence of creationism and evolution carving a possible future(That's right. For all the philosophical tone that this one has, it's a sci-fi) that will eventually make panentheists happy :)<br /><br />And chances are, at the rate at which humans deplete resources and hunt newer ones down, this story will make total sense even after a 100 years or may be more, but not forever [hopefully].<br /><br />There! That's one possible bunch of inferences that you can make from this sci-fi, all of them being equally scary and containing some bitter truths in varying forms. Go ahead and make your own. It's a tea-time read. If nothing else, it might serve as a warning bell to few pressing issues that are awaiting us[the Homo sapiens] in the future. It will leave you with intriguing questions as well. <br /><br />P.S.: There is a huge possibility of expecting too much from this story, if you hear about this story through someone. Just go back in time, and pick this book up accidentally. Hypes do not serve the cause of this piece of fiction.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1757973476326701939" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3029682172" />
    <a title="~Bookishly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/28323108-bookishly"><img alt="~Bookishly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605967142p2/28323108.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

This was an interesting short story, and I do admit, it's thought-provoking, but it wasn't really something I'd go out of my way to read. I would certainly recommend this to fans of science-fiction, though. </span>

  <span id="freeText17258995647862663175" style="display:none">This is most likely Isaac Asimov's best story. It appeared in the November 1956 issue of <i>Science Fiction Quarterly</i> and Asimov said [when he read his own stories: <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?t=15&amp;v=R3U30wSAV4Q" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>) that "it is just about my favorite story of all the stories I have written." <br /><br />It is certainly a superb story on the nature of entropy and the ultimate question: Can entropy be reversed? The twist which provides the answer comes in the final lines of the story and is stunning. There is little more that I can say without completely giving the story away. If you like Asimov, if you like good science fiction, if you like a blazing good story...just read it. Or, as I did, listen to it. I listened to Isaac Asimov read it. But my preferred version is the reading by Leonard Nimoy (<a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=rjqjSP7kOO4" rel="nofollow noopener">HERE</a>).</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3633090179621911080">We all have that moment where we decide to look a wee-bit further than our bedroom window, past the sky, past the galaxy, and into the gaping expanse universe. It's a feeling that doesn't ping on your daily radar, but Isaac Asimov's <i>The Last Question</i> is the go-to when you decide to tackle the <b>big</b> questions of the universe. <br/><br/><b>Entropy</b> is not just another pesky law you had to learn in your high-school chemistry class, but an overarching principle that governs our universe. So can we reverse the law? </span>
  <span id="freeText3633090179621911080" style="display:none">We all have that moment where we decide to look a wee-bit further than our bedroom window, past the sky, past the galaxy, and into the gaping expanse universe. It's a feeling that doesn't ping on your daily radar, but Isaac Asimov's <i>The Last Question</i> is the go-to when you decide to tackle the <b>big</b> questions of the universe. <br /><br /><b>Entropy</b> is not just another pesky law you had to learn in your high-school chemistry class, but an overarching principle that governs our universe. So can we reverse the law? <b>As to the answer, the last two sentences of this short story will blow your mind. And who doesn't want that every now and then?!</b></span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/731021151" />
    <a title="Alfaniel Aldavan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23645420-alfaniel-aldavan"><img alt="Alfaniel Aldavan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1382764468p2/23645420.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23645420-alfaniel-aldavan?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23645420-alfaniel-aldavan?shelf=cosmology">cosmology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer731021151" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2353580952138610975"><br/>The Last Question is a beautiful and engaging short story. Whether you pick reading its 20+ pages, or listen to an audio recording, I recommend you don't miss this little gem.<br/><br/>Asimov gives a visual account of a cosmological theory, an answer to the question "how to reverse entropy?", or the wonder of creation of an universe at all.<br/><br/>You can find it online for free.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amanda NEVER MANDY" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amanda NEVER MANDY" href="/user/show/36873041-amanda-never-mandy">Amanda NEVER MANDY</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36873041-amanda-never-mandy?shelf=2015">2015</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1446213107" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5039786710288852178">Great short story. I’m glad I took a moment to track it down and read it. It brought to mind another one that I read recently. They both were generally in the same ballpark theme-wise, but all bets were off when it came to quality. <b>MASSIVE</b> difference in my mind. When I finished this one I instantly thought, “…and that is how you’re supposed to do it!”</span>

          </span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('q2PBPl+TbJ+kO7xCB3rL7gBUR/PwczqvkLV30pye5/s25NJe9b7VYR4fHEjUJBBLmOSWAVgjFVupp+RDXN8EyA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('hIPoOYrQ+THPyUiKFb3ngpdWOT3MP1t7OdVfjrd/Dj8ZBPtZIP1Az3Xt6IDG4zwnD+boz2RvdI8Ax8wfdz7tDA==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/16667.Isaac_Asimov">About Isaac Asimov</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/16667.Isaac_Asimov"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1341965730p3/16667.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/16667.Isaac_Asimov">Isaac Asimov
</a></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer618330007857004456">Isaac Asimov was a Russian-born, American author, a professor of biochemistry, and a highly successful writer, best known for his works of science fiction and for his popular science books.<br/><br/>Professor Asimov is generally considered one of the most prolific writers of all time, having written or edited more than 500 books and an estimated 90,000 letters and postcards. He has works published in nine o</span>
  <span id="freeText618330007857004456" style="display:none">Isaac Asimov was a Russian-born, American author, a professor of biochemistry, and a highly successful writer, best known for his works of science fiction and for his popular science books.<br /><br />Professor Asimov is generally considered one of the most prolific writers of all time, having written or edited more than 500 books and an estimated 90,000 letters and postcards. He has works published in nine of the ten major categories of the Dewey Decimal System (lacking only an entry in the 100s category of Philosophy).<br /><br />Asimov is widely considered a master of the science-fiction genre and, along with Robert A. Heinlein and Arthur C. Clarke, was considered one of the "Big Three" science-fiction writers during his lifetime. Asimov's most famous work is the Foundation Series; his other major series are the Galactic Empire series and the Robot series, both of which he later tied into the same fictional universe as the Foundation Series to create a unified "future history" for his stories much like those pioneered by Robert A. Heinlein and previously produced by Cordwainer Smith and Poul Anderson. He penned numerous short stories, among them "Nightfall", which in 1964 was voted by the Science Fiction Writers of America the best short science fiction story of all time, a title many still honor. He also wrote mysteries and fantasy, as well as a great amount of nonfiction. Asimov wrote the Lucky Starr series of juvenile science-fiction novels using the pen name Paul French.<br /><br />Most of Asimov's popularized science books explain scientific concepts in a historical way, going as far back as possible to a time when the science in question was at its simplest stage. He often provides nationalities, birth dates, and death dates for the scientists he mentions, as well as etymologies and pronunciation guides for technical terms. Examples include his Guide to Science, the three volume set Understanding Physics, and Asimov's Chronology of Science and Discovery.<br /><br />Asimov was a long-time member and Vice President of Mensa International, albeit reluctantly; he described some members of that organization as "brain-proud and aggressive about their IQs" He took more joy in being president of the American Humanist Association. The asteroid 5020 Asimov, the magazine Asimov's Science Fiction, a Brooklyn, NY elementary school, and two different Isaac Asimov Awards are named in his honor.<br /><br />----------------------------------------<br /><i>Isaac Asimov. (2007, November 29). In Wikipedia, The Free Encyclopedia. Retrieved 21:50, November 29, 2007, from <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isaac_Asimov">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isaac_As...</a></i></span>
  <a data-text-id="618330007857004456" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
